STRIDING PUPILS OF THE SAN JOSE HIGH SCHOOL STARTING FOR THE SANTO
CLARA INSTITUTION.
1.00 pupils from the city and Its suburbs
Will .attend th;it Bchool.
The Interest of the entire city was cen
ta this morning's events and when
the time came for the pupils to leave for
Santa Clara there was a large crowd in
front of Turn Verein Hal!. The pupils be
x-.n gathering at about 8 o'clock and at 9
fully 100 were present to go to Santa
.Clara. About >.ie same number whose
parents oblige them to go to the local
school fame round and cheered the stu
dents and told them they were with them
in the movement, but for obvious reasons
could not K<i to Santa Clara. Tho parents
>jf some were dependent upon the gang
pport and dared not let their children
go. and other students said they would po
mta Clara after the election. One
young lady said her father compelled her
to the San Jnse school, but she was
E"intf to play '"hookey^ to-day in order to
give hf-r old' schoolmates a good send-off.
I tie students at Turn Verein Hall pre
si nted bappy countenances and they were
as fine and bright looking a lot of boys
and girls as could bÂ« seen in the State.
Tl â â >â¢ were enthusiastic, too, for they had
the best wishes of the entire community.
At 9 o'clock the students were called
together by Arthur Fruhling, their leader,
who addresssed them. He said the pupils
were lighting for a principle and would
Stick to it to the last. It was not because
of Professor Smith's dismissal they left
the school so much as because of the
principle Involved. There were evil influ
ences in the school and these were nurt
! Ing the institution. He said that at least
one-half the students were with them
! and he knew the entire community was.
! Students were cautioned to act like ladies
and gentlemen and thus make records at
their new school that would attract atten
tion to them.
The students then took seats In the
three large carryalls provided for them.
In these vehicles were eighty-two boys
I and girls, while twenty more accompa
nied them on wheels. The people gathered
about gave three cheers for the students.
I The carryalls made a tour of the business
! streets, the students giving their yells at
intervals. In front of Boss Rea's office
and the Mercury office these cries were
given with renewed vigor. People turned
out of stores and dwellings and wished
the students godspeed in the fight for
right.
About half an hour was pleasantly
passed in the ride to Santa Clara. The
I school song and yell were given over and
over. The quiet little town of Santa Clara
was awakened by this lively crowd and
j an enthusiastic welcome given the stu
| dents. At the High School were found
about twenty-five pupils from the suburbs
who had preceded them.
Fully a hundred residents of Santa
Clara 'had gathered at their school to wel
come the pupils from this city. The stu
dents piled out of the carryalls and
stormed the school. They were quickly
assigned to their rooms by Professor
Smith and in a half-hour all were seated
and there was no apparent sign of the
previous rush.
A count showed over 160 pupils in at
tendance against 63 for the last month of
the term of last year. Of the 160 fully 120
were former pupils of the San Jose school,
and it is expected that within a month the
number will reach 190. A number of the
students were absent to-day, and many
are working In the fruit orchards to se
cure money with which to pay their
transportation to Santa Clara and tuition
at the school.
At the High School in this city the
scene was in marked contrast to that at
Santa Clara. Here there was no joyful
ness, and students and teachers went
about in a sullen mood. Principal Shu
mate said 210 pupils had registered, of
which 96 were new pupils. The enrollment
last term was 341. In the senior class only
34 pupils reported. This is just oi.e-half
the number in the same* class last year.
Other classes show nearly the same ratio
of falling off. The enrollment of the other
classes are: Third year, 32: second year.
22; first year 24. Last year the average
number of pupils to a teacher was 50. but
this year it will not exceed 30. Principal
Shumate claims that the number of pu
pils will be increased. Knowing ones deny
this. and claim that young people only
visiting in this city were inducted to go to
the school and register as pupils to-day
to increase the attendance.
Business men view this exodus of
pupils to Santa Clara with alarm. It
means the loss of many thousands of dol
lars yearly. Formerly many families,
came here to live so that their children'
could attend the High School, but now
these have removed to Santa Clara. The
tuition paid to the local High School by
non-resident pupils amounted to nearly
$3000 last year. This amount will now go
to Santa Clara, as will probably the
trade of these students' families. Now
that the students are in earnest In this
strike, the business men are also loud
in denouncing "Boss" Rea and the City
Board of Education.
The gang has adopted every means pos
sible to checkmate the students In this
affair. The scholars have been called
into Rea's office and coaxed and threat
ened. Injunction proceedings have been
brought against the Santa Clara trustees
to impede them in furnishing the school,
and now City Superintendent Russell is
about to aid the gang in its work. Rus
sell has discovered in the school law that
there Is some section that prohibits pu
pils attending school in another district
without obtaining permission from the
trustees in the district previously at
tended. He has proposed to the trustees
that injunction proceedings be com
menced to compel the students to attend
the local school.
[ The people of Santa Clara are rejoiced
over the addition of now students to their
High School. David Henderson, presi
dent of the school board, said to-day that
the gang: had tried In every way to hin
der the school. The trustees did not fear
the injunction proceedings begun last
week to enjoin them from using certain
school funds to Improve the buildings.
He said that Rea was at the bottom of
the suit. No money had been Illegally
used, and District Attorney Herinpton
and City Attorney Beasley of San Jose
had a few months ago rendered such an
opinion.
Another trustee said Rea was so thor
oughly disliked in Santa Clara that
should he put In an appearance there
he would probably be run out.
This strike of the students Is a novel
one. and shows what politics will do to
wreck a school. Efficiency is no longer
regarded in the selection of teachers In
the city schools, and those with the larg
est political pulls are giv^n the best
places. Professor Smith's dismissal was
purely for political reasons. A day or
two before the charter election last
spring Smith in addressing his class on
municipal governments statr-d that one
man power was the ruination of cities,
This was contorted as an address against
the gang, and this was the prime cause
of his dismissal. The gang then had the
Battlefield Veterans, a political organ
ization that does Rea's dirty work, peti
tion the school board for a dismissal of
Smith on the ground that he was un
patriotic. They claimed he had stated
that "Grant would have dl^d a poor tan
ner if it had not been for the civil war,"
and they interpreted this as casting slurs
upon the great general. This was merely
done to divert attention from the real
cause, which was that he had uncon
sciously given the gang a hard rap in
teaching his class the evils of one-man
power in city affairs.
Tom Rea, a son of "Boss" Roa, did not
attend the local High School to-day.
Much speculation is indulged in as to
whether he intends to stay at home or
go to the Santa Clara school.

The following resolution was adopted
unanimously:
Whereas. A war In the cause of liberty be
tween the United States and Spain has been
brought to a &u<-cossiul conclusion and peace
will soon be declared; and
Whereas. The Young Men's Institute, tru" to
tits motto, "For God and for Country," was
one of the firÂ«t organizations to offer and
Klve Its services to the country: and
Whereas. The Pacific Jurisdiction of the
Young Men's Institute was represented in this
war by James F. Smith, our worthy past grand
president, colonel of the First California Vol
unteers; Rev. McKlnnon, our worthy past
grand vice president, chaplain of the First
Refdment of California Volunteers, and George
D. Payne, our worthy grand vice president, a
lieutenant in the Nevada Volunteers, and
thousands of other brave members of our order
who have enlisted in the service; therefore.
be It
Resolved, That we, the members of the
F<>urttÂ»enth Grand Council of the Young Men's
Inctltute. Pacific Jurisdiction, rejoice with all
Americans upon the success of American arms
upon land and sea and we congratulate William
McKlnleyi our respected President, upon the
able and humane manner In which the war
has been prosecuted and brought to a glorious
termination.

THE CZAR'S ENCYCLICAL.
NICHOLAS, the Czar and autocrat of all the
Russias, has met the reports of impending war
between his empire and any other nation by an
encyclical note addressed to the powers of Europe re
questing them to meet with his representatives in con
ference for the purpose of providing a way for the
maintenance of peace and the disbandment of the
large armies and fleets which now entail such a heavy
burden upon the people of all European countries.
The proposition was so startling that the first com
ments on it were hardly more than gasps of surprise.
A London critic calls it a dramatic stroke and says
its effect coming from the Czar was almost as great
as it it had come from the Kaiser at Jerusalem. In
Berlin it was said that the note would make a good
subject for discussion during the dull season. In
Paris they wondered how the Czar could propose dis
armament without first consulting France. In neither
of the three great capitals of Western Europe does
it appear that the proposal is regarded as at all prac
ticable. Everywhere it has been met with a cynical
incredulity and a criticism verging upon ridicule.
There are ample reasons for believing, however,
that the Czar is quite serious and that he will make a
really earnest and vigorous effort to arrange for peace
with the other European powers that will last at
least for the rest of this generation. He has nothing
to gain and much to lose by the outbreak of hostili
ties, and the cost of maintaining his army and navy
in readiness for war is a heavy burden indeed upon a
people so poor as those of his empire.
The ambitions of his Ministers, moreover, at this
time are not directed toward wars and military con
quests. They are now constructing a railway across the
vast Asiatic continent and are planning a canal from
the Baltic to the Black Sea. These enterprises entail
large expenditures, and his financiers would gladly be
relieved from the necessity of providing money not
only for them, but for an army and a navy besides.
It is furthermore to be remembered that the estab
lishment of the proposed peace would not in any way
check the advance of Russia in Asia nor prevent the
expansion of her empire. Now that she has obtained
from China control of a port on the Pacific that is
open all the year round she can afford to move slowly
and trust to diplomacy, colonization and commerce
to win for her all that she wishes in that part of the
world. As for the long desired extension southward
toward Constantinople, that is a matter that can wait.
In the meantime diplomacy can do much in that di
rection also and virtually make Turkey a dependency
of Russia.
The proposal of the Czar is therefore one well cal
culated to promote the interests of his empire. There
is no reason why he should not urge it with all his
force and influence. It is one that appeals to the
sympathies of good men, to the desires of the peas
antry of all countries to be relieved from heavy taxes
and military duties, to the interests of industry and
that great commercial class which has now become so
powerful in the world and finally it must appeal also
to the wisdom of statesmen who have been long
struggling with the complexities of the existing sit
uation. The coming conference, therefore, is likely
to be an important one. It has been met at first with
cynicism and ridicule, but it will be treated seriously
before long.
According to W. H. H. Hart, to himself is due the
fact that the President favors the retention of the
Philippines. Mr. Hart will be remembered as the
gentleman who glided into office on the strength of
having rallied a panic-stricken army by an heroic rata-
plan, and with his little drum leading them on to vie-
tory. The trifling circumstance that Hart had not
been on the scene was not permitted to interfere.
However, he may have converted the President. At
least there is no proof that he didn't.
â¢ Police Judges sometimes seem to get an idea that
they are collection agents and have a right to employ
criminal law for the expedition of civil business.
It is hardly necessary to state that they are mistaken
in this, and that when they strike a man with the
nerve to demonstrate the absurdity of their position
they will be brought up with a turn so sudden that it
will make their heads swim.
The doctor of a Kansas regiment has been sen
tenced to five years' imprisonment for having dese
crated the grave of a Confederate officer on the field
of Bull Run. The sentence does not at first glance
seem severe enough, but it must be considered that
the doctor will have to go back to Kansas to serve it.
Self-defense as an excuse for shooting an unarmed
man of 80 strikes the unbiased as being rather a weak
plea. However, it seems to be the best Cornelius Sul
livan can advance, and we have no disposition to in
terfere with the tribunals in which the administration
of justice is both an art and a profession.
An old-time bicycle champion was recently de
feated by a new racer, and the crowd hissed the loser.
It was an Eastern crowd, a fact which knocks the
spots out of the theory that civilization along the
Atlantic border is of a superior type.
A WORD WITH DEMOCRATS.
IT is evident that all the political fusions of this year,
made by the Bryan leaders in control of the Dem
ocratic party, are made to secure a show of strength
that will lead to Bryan's nomination in 1900 as a sort
of forlorn but star-spangled hope.
The New York Democracy has chafed under the
conditions of 1896. It elected last year a Cleveland
Democrat to the Court of Appeals and another Mayor
of New York City.
This is regarded as high and mighty treason by the
Bryan leaders, who now serve notice on the Knicker
bockers that they must this year indorse the Chicago
platform and dance the whole silver figure or take the
consequences.
It is interesting to note the paths into which fusion
is leading these people who follow a name and
tremble in the presence of "regularity" in conventions
and tickets.
In every fusion made this year the party commits
itself to an issue of irredeemable paper currency. In
California the strongest party in the fusion trinity is
undoubtedly the Populist. Take away from the Dem
ocratic ticket the gold or national Democrats, who
accept the Indianapolis platform as expressive of the
true faith, and the others who don't believe in fusion,
and the Popuiists outnumber both the Democrats and
silver Republicans. They were first in the field to
nominate Judge Maguire, and he has signed and ap
proved their platform, and declares that it is identical
with the Democratic platform.
That platform demands free silver at a ratio of 16 to
i, but it also, in more decisive tones, demands that the
Government issue irredeemable paper currency in un
limited volume and arrange for equitably distributing
the same among the people. Every man who votes
the fusion ticket votes for this paper issue. In the
stress and emergency of the Civil War the Republican
party was driven into the issue of the greenback paper
currency. The Government agreed to make that cur
rency good by converting it into money. But this
promise to redeem it did not prevent its fluctuation,
and the Democrats never tired of attacking it and the
party that was responsible for it. We suppose that the
party now will take strictly Democratic testimony as
to the effect of that paper currency upon labor and
tiade.
In 1864 there were two organs of Democratic
opinion in the country which never struck their flags
and held out to the end. One of these was "The Old
Guard," edited in New York by C. Chauncey Burr
and Thomas Dunn English. That magazine dictated
Democratic opinion. It said in 1864: "The issue of
any kind of paper promise to circulate as money is a
fraud upon the producer, because the tendency is to
give him less of moneys worth than he would get if
paid in specie. It is also a cheat to rob the working
man of the proceeds of his daily labor. This is un
doubtedly the effect of the paper system; but its first
effect is to cause an appearance of prosperity, which
lasts a little while, until revulsion ensues."
There being then $600,000,000 of paper circulation,
"The Old Guard" said: "This is a pretty abundant
currency, and the fact that it is so is manifest every
day in its depreciation. For instance, in Canada flour
is $4 25 per barrel in coin. Ten miles south of the
Canada line the same flour is $6 75 per barrel in paper.
The consumer is robbed of $2 50 by the depreciation
of the paper, showing its superabundance. The Gov
ernment is jeopardized by the infamous schemers who
seek through paper money to destroy not only the
Union, but what remains of popular rights, even the
right to live by labor."
If these were the direful effects of issuing a re
deemable paper currency, we leave it to the Demo
crats of to-day to say what will follow the issue of
irredeemable paper demanded by their candidate for
Governor.
"The Old Guard" continued its discussion of paper
currency, saying: "High wages do not long delude
the poor man, for he finds them to be very low wages
when he goes into the market to buy the necessaries
of life. He discovers that his paper abolition dollar
buys less than 37 per cent of what old-fashioned Dem
ocratic money used to. There is an average increase
of 45 per cent in the price of everything the poor man
must buy with the money he receives for his labor,
while the nominal increase in the price of labor is less
that 10 per cent, which leaves the workingman poorer
by 35 per cent. And prices are still going up, while
the value of the Republican currency is running
down. Let this policy continue three years longer
and the poor of this country will be brought to the
door of starvation."
In all that there was the element of a general truth.
The price of everything except labor, in an inflated
but redeemable paper currency, did rise as the cur
rency fell. The Republican party, mindful of the con
ditions that were created in the emergencies of war,
immediately prepared to redeem its promise of con
verting greenbacks into money, and made them worth
their face in gold.
But, we ask every reasonable Democrat if redeem
able paper currency brought the industrial disasters
of which they complained in 1864, what will be the
effect of the irredeemable currency which they are
supporting in their fusions of 1898?
CUBfTS PECULIAR PATRIOTS.
IF Dr. Castillo is a sample Cuban patriot the island
will be in a bad way after it has really fallen into
the hands of the natives. Yankees have been
termed "pigs," but if the appellation is deserved, what
shall the Castillos be called? This chap is in Wash
ington with a claim for $.15,000 for the destruction of
cocoanut trees near Siboney. He is not willing to
take his pay in the boon of liberty; he is after the
cash, and deserves not to get it.
Castillo does not state the manner in which his trees
were destroyed. Perhaps the boys in shinning them
for nuts barked them fatally. Possibly the trunks
were needed for the construction of roads. It may be
they were scorched to death when necessity arose for
burning Siboney to destroy the yellow fever germs
lurking there. But whatever the precise circum
stances, Castillo has not a good case. If it were a
first-class case his grandchildren might collect, but
there is no utility in Castillo's monkeying around
with his claim.
Americans did not go to Cuba with the idea of de
stroying anything but Spanish rule. That they suc
ceeded in this more than offsets the incidental dam
age. Castillo did not draw a sword himself, nor, in
deed, draw more than regular rations. So long as
he preserved a whole skin and a full stomach he ought
to be satisfied. It's a measley sort of patriot who is
willing to have an alien nation do his fighting and fur
nish his grub, and is not willing to donate to the
cause a few trees. Probably the trees were not worth
the sum mentioned, but if Castillo pins his faith to the
cocoanut as a source of income, his best scheme is
to go back to Cuba and plant some more.
This Castillo is a brother of the general who
imagines himself rightful Governor of Santiago, and
who is likely to have his material system materially
jarred unless he lay aside his pretensions. The first
the Castillo family knows it will be in disfavor.
While Dewey is calling for more ships there can
hardly be an absolute certainty that Merritt may not
call for some more men.
THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL., TUESDAY, AUGUST 30, 1898.
THE LEGISLATIVE TICKET.
I I ARDLY less important to California Republi-
I I cans in this campaign than the selection of
* * strong candidates for State offices is that of
putting forward in every county a strong legislative
ticket. The issues involved in the control of the
coming Legislature are far reaching and are likely to
affect national as well as State politics.
There are at present eighty-nine members of the
United States Senate, Oregon being one Senator
short, and of these forty-four are Republicans. The
recent victory in Oregon assures that the vacancy in
the Senatorial representation from that State will be
filled by a Republican, and a Republican has also been
chosen to succeed Senator Gorman of Maryland. If
no other changes occur the party will have just a bare
majority in the Senate When the next Congress as
sembles, and that margin of superiority will hardly be
sufficient for the important legislation that is ex
pected to be undertaken. It is clear, therefore, that in
order to advance the adoption of the great national
policies of the Republican party witli regard to the
currency, the promotion of American shipping ami
other hardly less important matters, the Republicans
of California should make an earnest effort to send a
Republican to the Senate to succeed Senator White.
In addition to the national issues which may be de
pendent upon the vote of the Senator to be elected at
Sacramento this winter, there are a host of local
questions which render it imperative that the Re
publicans should have a strong working majority in
the Legislature. No matter what advantages nature
may have bestowed upon a particular community, its
prosperity is always more or less affected by the laws
which direct its affairs. No natural prosperity, how
ever great, can withstand the drain put upon a people
by a wasteful and extravagant Legislature if left to
run its inevitable course.
While the large national questions at stake upon the
election of a United States Senator, and the impor
tant local issues involved in the election of a Legisla
ture that will be at once economical and progressive,
will naturally incline men to vote the Republican leg
islative ticket, too much dependence should not be
placed upon those factors in the problem. Senatorial
and Assembly districts are comparatively small, and
the candidates in them are generally so well known
to the voters that personal considerations are often as
effective as political issues in determining results on
election day. The legislative ticket, therefore, should
be made up of men who are personally popular and
esteemed; men whom the people know and trust;
men who are not under the domination or influence
of corporations or bosses; men who are not pledged
or bound to any corrupting combination or personal
ity; men who can be counted on to do in office that
which they promise in the campaign, and whose can
didacy will add strength to the whole ticket in the
districts where they run.
THE SILVER "REPUBLICANS."
AMONG the resurrected political fragments
which go to make up the Maguire fusion party
none occupy quite so humiliating a position as
the Silver Republicans. Representatives of these laction
ists met in State convention at Los Angeles on Friday
last, adopted a platform and meekly accepted the mor
sel of patronage doled out to them by the fusion com
mittee which conducted the Populist and Democratic
conventions. But there was no enthusiasm, and no
interest, no exultant feeling of victory, no cheering â
nothing, in fact, except a feast of indigestible crow.
This may have been due to the circumstance that
out of the fusion division the Silver Republicans have
obtained but two offices. One of these is a Justice of
the Supreme Court who is without political pull or
patronage, and who cannot even stump the State â at
least, not without some sacrifice of judicial dignity and
neglect of duty. Walter Van Dyke, the nominee re
ferred to, is a Superior Judge of Los Angeles County.
The other crumb doled out to the Silver Republicans
was the clerkship of the Supreme Court â a position
which has been given to H. A. McCraney, an old line
stalwart, the better part of whose life has been ex
pended in scalping Democrats and putting Populists
to the torture.
But the Silverites have accepted what has been
given to them with thanks, and in return they have
swallowed the entire fusion ticket, unwashed and all.
How many of them will vote for the candidates other
than Van Dyke and McCraney remains to be seen.
If one may judge from the platform adopted by the
convention not many of them will do that. The
Silver Republican declaration "reaffirms" the prin
ciples and doctrines of the Republican party "as de
clared and promulgated by Abraham Lincoln and
James G. Elaine." These principles and doctrines do
not accord with those "declared and promulgated" by
Thomas Jefferson, who was set up by the Democratic
convention as the political deity of the day. How
can the Silver Republicans support a ticket which
represents in great part the antithesis of Republican
ism? How can they vote for nominees who regard
the Dingley law as the invention of the devil, seeing
that that law establishes the great principles for
which Rlaine contended all his life? How can they
vote for nominees who look upon McKinley as an
"enemy" while they commend him for his "earnest
ness and patriotic zeal in the prosecution of the war?"
On one point, however, the Silver Republicans
agree with their fellow fusionists. They are united on
the 16 to I theory, on their opposition to the retire
ment of greenbacks, on their aversion for bonds and
denunciation of international bimetallism, and on their
view that bankers are birds of prey who feed upon the
remains of the people. But all this has an ancient and
fish-like smell. Are the Silver Republicans aware of
the fact that these things were repudiated by the peo
ple in November in 1896, and have since been in
terred in the political graveyard? Evidently the poli
ticians of the Silver Republican party have just re
covered from a long nap, and yet their platform indi
cates that they are actually aware of the war with
Spain and have an inkling of the new issues which
have in consequence sprung up and which are now
engaging the minds nf men.
The only hope for the Silver Republicans, however,
is to keep awake. If they fall into slumber again they
may open their eyes after election and find themselves
dead.
Now all the officers of the police force above the
rank of patrolman want to be provided with swords.
It might have been known that something of this sort
would happen when the Chief insisted on riding at
the head of every procession with a glittering blade
spanking the flanks of his charger. He looked pretty
enough to be demoralizing.
In attempting to draw a parallel between the fa
mous Barnaby poisoning case in Colorado and the
Dunning tragedy the Chronicle might have added that
the man convicted of the former crime was convicted
on evidence on which a sensible jury would not have
hanged a yellow dog.
Sagasta declares that Spain is still at war. The
Premier has evidently become so rattled that he does
not know peace when he sees it

DEATH OF COLONEL
JOHN O'BYRNE
DISTINGUISHED ARISH-AMEBI
CAN PASSES AWAY.
A Man of Scholarly Attainments and
Great Versatility Who /lad uu
Interesting Career.
Colonel John O" Byrne died at Â«!:#*
o'clock last evening at his home. l- air Oaks
and Twenty-fourth streets, after an 111â
nr-s.s of several weeks, during which he
Buffered the most Intense pain. Th< .-ause
of his death was Inflammatory rheuma
tism. which went to his heart.
Colonel O'Byrne was one of San Fran
cisco's most prominent and distinguished
citizens, and as a leader of Irish-Ameri
cans he had a national reputation. His
history la an Interesting one. He was
bora in Dublin in 1825, four years before
the passage of the O'Connell emancipa
tion act. His ancestors wi re the
<ri:, rues, chieftains of Wi<klow. and he
was very proud of this fart. After re
ceiving a thorough education he at once
plunged into political affairs, and at the
; .};.â  of 2:; was a participant i:i the Irish
rebellion of 1848. Making his escape, he
came to the United States, locating in
New York City, where lie engaged in
the field of journalism for a few years.
Colonel O'Byrne then bored to Phila
delphia and there studied law in the of
iice of Benjamin F. Brewster, who subse
quently was Attorney General of the
United States. In the year 1864 O'Byrne
took a prominent part in the defense of
the Catholic- people in the Know-Nothing
riots.
When the Civil War broke out he went
to the front for the Union, serving in one
of the Philadelphia regiments commanded
by Colonel Gosland.
About th- year 1870 Colonel O'Byrne
purchased In Urandywine Hundred, a sub
urb of Wilmington, Delaware, a beautiful
and extensive estate, where he lived for
several years. Buying the Weekly Adver
tiser of Wilmington he converted it into
a daily morning paper under the name of
the Herald. This was the first morning
paper in the State of Delaware.
At the request of the Democratic Na
tional Committee Colonel O'Byrne stump
ed the State of New York in the presiden
tial campaign of ISSO for General Hancock.
Hia experience in that campaign led him
to change his residence from Delaware to
New York, where he was soon appointed
assistant to the District Attorney, Hon.
John McKeon. the distinguished anti-
Tammany Judge. Colonel O'Byrne filled
this responsible position with great effi
ciency and success. Owing to failing
health he came to California in 1889, and
was soon recognized as a lawyer of great
ability and an orator of magnetic elo
quence. He was the orator of the day at
the St. Patrick's day celebration in 1S:<O,
and on that occasion delivered a brilliant
and memorable oration.
Colonel O'Bryneâ journalist, lawyer, or
ator, soldier, patriot â was a man of schol
arly attainments and great versatility.
He could address a political audience and
deliver a lecture before an astronomical
or geographical society the same even
ing, or engage in the defense of a person
charged with crime. His w:is a m"?t ac
tive life, and he was constantly in the
midst of political excitement. He was,
however, a man wh<> never carried for a
day any malice or revengeful vindictive
ness even against his most aggrtj.-.-iva
and mdHt bitter opponent.
Colonel O'Byrne was a patriotic man
both as an American and as an Irish
man. He was a participant In the fa
mous Fenian movement, and was the
last of the charter members of the cele
brated Philopatrian Society of Philadel
i phla. He also fok an active part in the
i Land league movement, headed by Par
nell.
Colonel O Byrne was a member of the
Knights of St. Patrick, Ancient Order
of Hibernians. Knights of the Red
1 Branch and other Irish societies. He
a widow, four sons and one daugh
! ter. One of his sons is a lawyer In this
city, and another is with the Sixty-ninth
, New Y<>rk Regiment. Th*- daughter Is
j a Visitation Nun in Philadelphia.
TWO SUDDEN DEATHS.
Martin W. Meyerhof , . Grocer, and
Daniel Hutchinson, Milkman, '
the Victims. .â  â¢| â¢ |
Martin IK. *Meyerhof , proprietor â  of the *
grocery "at 'the corner of Mason and Geary
streets, fell dead at 6 o'clock yesterday'
morning, while engaged in opening his
place of business. He was 41 years old.
An Inquest will be waived by Coroner
Hawkins, as there is no doubt that heart
disease was the cause of death.
Apoplexy: carried off Daniel Hutchinse*
at 9:45 o'clock yesterday morning, at ms
residence on the southeast corner of
Pierce and Filbert streets. Hutchjnson
was a retired milkman, 65 years old, a
native of Connecticut. There will be no
inquest in his case.
Died From His Injuries.
A. L. Foreman, at one time a celebrated
astronomer of New York, but for the past
fifteen years a resident of this city, died
in St. Luke's Hospital yesterday.
The deceased, who was in his fifty-sec
ond year, received injuries about one year
ago while riding on a Sutro electric car
from which be never fully recovered.
These injuries brought on others of a

JOIN HANDS TO ELECT GAGE
TROUBLE IN STORE FOR THE
DEMOCRATIC NOMINEE.
Mr. Maguire Is to Be Informed
That He Must Take Care of
Others Besides the
Gallaghers.
An. eloquent company assembled last
evening in the law offices of General W.
H. L. Barnes. Republican orators who are
known to the masses as "spoil binders"
convened by appointment to meet Henry
T. Gage, the Republican nominee for
Governor, and exchange views with the
Btandard-bearer of the party on the topic
c|f campaign speeches.
/The orators present were: Joseph C.
Campbell, George A. Knight. Van R. Fat
erson, \Y. H. L. Barnes and Judge Car
penter. Some significance is attached to
the fact that two of the eloquent speak
ers are candidates for the United States
Senate to succeed Hon. Stephen M.
White.
The conference was a happy ono
throughout. Before the State convention
met Messrs. Knight, Campbell, Barnes
and Paterson were not advocates of Mr.
Gage's nomination, but iast night they
declared without reservation and with
enthusiasm that the nominee of the
convention should have their earnest, ac
tfve and open support until the ballots
were counted. Every one agreed to take
.the stump and speak for the Republican
clause and the Republican ticket.
. Mr. Gage submitted to them his ideas
or the line of battle, and they made such
suggestions as their judgment dictated.
The utmost good feeling prevailed from
the time the conference convened until it
dissolved.
Mr. Gage will leave San Francisco in
time to reach Los Angeles next Saturday
evening and attend the great home dem
onstration which his neighbors and par
ty friends are preparing in his honor. Be
fore leaving here he will submit the
names of twenty-one men to comprise
the executive committee of the Republi
can State Central Committee. Many of
...imes have been selected, and it was
paid by Mr. Gage last night that the en
tire list would be announced before he
left the city for his home.
When Judge Maguiro returns from the
South to-day he will find awaiting him
tuch a. budget of protests as will cause
him to awake from his dream and come
to a realization of the fact that if he
wishes to be elected Governor he will
have to consider other things than the
promotion of the single tax doctrine and
the betterment of the political condition
(if the members of the Clan Gallagher.
The Immediate cause of the rude awak
ening that awaits the much-pledged Dera
ii,' candidate for Governor is the
proposed personnel of the executive com
mUte-o of the Democratic State Central
O'ommittee. The Gallaghers, the Manns
and the Leggetts have formed a close
corporation and have named Maguire as
the mouthpiece through which is an
nounced the law as regards the conduct
of the present campaign. In this manner
It has been given to the world that tho
executive; commit toe is to be composed of
Gallaghers ana single tax-rs with Selh
Mann at the helm. The men who have

the "party have waited in vain for a

YOUNG REPUBLICANS RALLY.
A Rousing Meeting Held in the
Forty-First District.
Lively music and eloquent speeches
were listened to by a large number of
Republicans In Lougheau Hall, 1605 Polk
last night during the initial meet
ing of the Young Men's Independent Re
publican. Club of the Forty-first District.
P. D. Worth, President of the Conti
nental Republican Club, acted as tem
porary chairman, and in a neat speech
introduced Major C W. Kyle as the first
speaker of the evening. The major as
sured his listeners that by voting the Re
publican ticket they were voting fur a
ticket, which had representative citizens
as' candidates, and it was for their good
that they elect them to office. He then
spoke of Henry T. (ingo, the next Gov
ernor of California. The speaker closed
with a eulogy of the Republican candidate
and- told with what veneration he was
held by the people of the south, of his
ability as a lawyer and of his qualities as
a man.
Colonel John H. Roberts was then intro
duced. "We are just starting on a cam
paign," he said. "It is not going to be a
walkover. We are going to have enough
to do. We can only arrive at a .just con
clusion by havinr a knowledge of the
past." He paid his respects to Mr. Ma-
Kuire. and charged him with opposing the
passage of a tax on beer, tobacco, bonds
.and mortgages and stamps on checks as
I'eing hardships on the poor man. and of
) is opposing the annexation of the Ha
walian Islands for fear that an Influx of
Chinese would flow into this country and
revolutionize labor clrc!'
I "hidrnian Worth followed with a short
speech, in which he told the members to
get out and hustle, as there was good
yoilng" mat* rial In the district that needed
cultivation. Mr. Ballard followed in the
same strain.
51. H. Bheppard was elected permanent
chairman of the club and Charles Kend
ricks secretary.
It was stated that Mr. Gage would be in
vited to address the club some time next
month. . Â»

THE DEMOCRATS
WILL NOT HOLD
ANY PRIMARY
Followers of Phelan
Afraid of a Contest.
WILL APPOINT DELEGATES
STRUGGLING TO DISRUPT THE
HARNEYITES.
There Are Many Candidetes in the
Field Hoping to Be Struck
by the Political Light
ning Rod.
The suggestion made by the Democratic
State Convention, that a primary election
be held in this city to settle the differ
ences between the Harneyites and the
Sullivan-Phelan-McNab wing of the local
Democracy will nut be accepted. TII9
Phelan-McNab contingent will not meet
the Harneyites in a primary battle. They
are afraid of the result. They believe
that "a bird in the hand is worth two in
the bush," and are not going to take any
chances of losing the bird they now hold.
In other words, McNab and Phelan be
j lieve they now have full control of the
local political machine and consequently
are unwilling to risk the chance of los
ing it.
The Committee of One Hundred will not
call a primary for the election of dele
gates to the municipal nominating con
vention. The Harneyites are only too anx
ious to meet them at a primary, but they
will not be given an opportunity to show
their strength. The leaders of the Com
mittee of One Hundred have decided since
their return from Sacramento that they
have everything to lose by holding a pri
mary. They argue on the line that their
appointed delegates to the State conven
tion were recognized and that the dele
gates they will appoint to that municipal
convention will also be recognized. If
both factions were to go out of existence
and a primary were held to elect dele
gates to the municipal convention and
also elect a new county committee, it is
feared by the Sullivan-Phelan-JUcNab
people that they would be defeated* at the
polls. Under these circumstances they
will ignore the suggestion of the State
convention and appoint a municipal con
vention that will L>e composed of men who
will programme on every office.
The plan of the self-oppointed bosses is
to have a municipal nominating conven
tion of 200 members. The Committee of
One Hundred will form one-half of the
convention and the other 100 will be se
lected by them in about the same manner
as the delegates to the State convention
were chosen. They are of the opinion
that with a majority of the Board of
Election Commissioners to sustain the
programme, they will not experience any
dimeulty in having their nominees placed
,on the ballot as the nominees of the Dem
ocratic party. But to guard against any
break, they are now working on the mem
bers of the Hartley committee of 460. (jilt- I
edge promises of political preferment are j
being made to the vacillating members ]
of the Harney committee to have them
vote to adjourn the Harney committee
sine die.
There is every indication that there will
be a "hot time" in the Democratic camp
next week.- The committees of both fac
tions will meet, and the struggle for su
premacy will commence. The Phelan-
McNab faction feels confident that it will
be able to secure votes enough": to disrupt
the Harney committee. In that event it
will have clear sailing so far as the mat
ter of nominations is concerned. If the
leaders carry out their scheme It is al
most safe to say that the local Demo
cratic ticket will be snowed under on ;
election day. The Harneyites will stand ;
by the State and Congressional ticket, but
will stop there. If the McNab-Phelanltea 1
succeed in disrupting the Harney com- |
mittee they will not be able to weld the
party together again. Not a few of the
Harneyites say that they fought to the |
laat to receive proper recognition, and j
failing in that they will vote the local
Republican ticket rather than submit to
the dictation of the self-appointed bosses.
Neither of the committees will meet be- j
fore the middle of next week, unless there
is a change In the programme. The Har
neyites are awaiting the action of the i
newly appointed State Central Committee,
and are in hopes that it may decide to
take a firm stand in the matter anf
order a primary for the election of dele- |
gates to the municipal convention. In
that event they will be satisfied; other
wise they will give battle to the local
ticket if chosen by an appointed conven-
Mayor Phelan -will be renominated by
his faction for Mayor. lie- would like to
step out, however, and wait until the new
charter goes into effect He would then
run for Mayor and become the boss of the
so-called civil service reform movement if
elected Hut he cannot get out of tne
present struggle. To withdraw wou.d
probably result in the loss of the political
machine a year from now, and he does not
want to lose anything he has on hand
that will aid him in reaching the guber
natorial chair, which he ia looking upon
with jealous eye.
If the Phelan-McNab plan does not fail.
Judges Borden. Seawall and Wallace will
be renominated and the tourth place will
go to cither Frank Murasky or Stephen
V. Coatello.
Senator Mead is out against Public Ad
ministrator Freese for that office, and
Franklin K. Lane and James Gallagher
will contest for the nomination for City
and County Attorney. Gallagher, who be
longs to the Gallagher-Maguire clan, be
lieves that he has the inside track, by
reason of the fact that he now holds the
office, having been appointed by the Su
pervisors when Harry Creswell resigned.
License Collector Bonnet, ex-County

& Co., are mentioned for the Democratic
nomination for County Clerk.
Sheriff Whelan would like another term
in office, and up to date he lias a good
fight on hand for the Democratic nomina
tion.
Tax Collector Block is said to have the
inside track for the Democratic nomina
tion for Assessor, and Eugene N. Dcu
prey is looked upon as the Democratic
slate candidate for District Attorney. If
Block is nominated for Assessor, Hiram
B. Cook, his chief deputy, will probably
carry oft the nomination for Tax Col
lector.
Recorder Tom Glynn is not satisfied
with a four-year term in office and ex

Phelanites. Ex-Senato* Thomas Pinder
will try conclusions with him for the
nomination.
The Republican county committee will
meet in the early part of next week and

to the municipal convention, which will
convene not later than September 15.

THE SA:N FRANCISCO CALL, TUESDAY, AUGUST 30, 1898.
FINEST AMERICAN STEEL SHIP AFLOAT.
The Erskine M. Phelps, now loading at Baltimore for San Francisco, is American from stem to stern
and from keel to truck. She was launched from the Sewall yards last July, and should arrive here next Feb
ruary. Captain Graham, who is well known nere as master of the W. F. Babcock, will command the new
crack.
COME FROM
ALL DISTRICTS
Minor Offices Causing
Unusual Strife.
CHURCH HAS COMPETITORS
AN ARMY OF ASPIRANTS TO
DISPENSE JUSTICE.
Constables' Fees Also Attract a
Large Number of Candidates
Fusion Appears to Be
in Favor.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
906 Broadway, August 29.
As there appears to be something in the
nature of a slate in connection with coun
ty offices, more interest than usual cen
ters around the district contests in which
it is apparent that so far no programme
exists.
Two Senators, Wo Supervisors, six As
semblymen and a myriad of Justices of
the Peace and Constables all over the
county have to be voted on at the coming
election, and a score of young lawyers
and as many active politicians are after
the nominations.
To succeed Senator Denlson Assembly
man Frank M. Leavitt and District At
torney Snook are the only candidates.
Leavitt conns from the oixth and Snook
from the Fourth Ward. In the latter
ward there are several candidates, but in
the Sixth there are practically none at
this time, and, of course, the smaller
number from any district increases the
chances ot its aspirants. In the Fourth
Ward Will 11. Church is a candidate for
Supervisor to succeed himself. He has a
very strong opponent in Joseph K. Kelly,
and a strong effort is being made by
Church's friends to pull Kelly down with
a promise of support should he run for
the Assembly. Kelly is not listening with
any too willing ear to this promise, and
at present shows no inclination to forego
hiÃ supervisorial ambition.
Dr. Simpson of Alameda, who some
years ago was prominent in politics, has
once more come forward ajid he also
would like the nomination for Supervisor,
the island being also in Church's district.
Supervisor Talcott is not yet threatened
with any opposition, no one having an
nounced that he Intends to compete with
him for the nomination. Roeth, Mitchell
and Wells hold over.
For tne Assembly nominations there is
much scrambling. In the Fifty-first Dis
trict Howard E. Wright wants to go back
to Sacramento, but William B. Pringle
and ex-Councilman C. G. Dodge are also
anxious to represent the district, and
each is looking around to see what com
binations can be made to further his
chances of success.
The Forty-eighth Assembly District
presents an open fleiu for a fight. Phil
M. Walsh, Frank Burnett, Will Friend
and others have been mentioned, but no
active work has so far been done by any
of them. In the other districts no formal
announcements of candidates have yet
been made, except by A. A. McKeen, who
would like to be the nominee from the
Forty-ninth.
Hut the great struggle for place is on in
the contest for Justices of the Peace of
Oakland Township. Justice Clift Is to re
tire and hopes to be District Attorney,
and for his seat have already appeared a
small army. Constable Cronin was the
first to express his desire to be a Justice,
and alter him came John W. Stetson, who
was nominated four years ago and beaten
by Fred Wood, who ran Independently.
Will D. Powers. A. W. Bishop and Judge
Talcott complete the list, while for city
Justices Harry Pulcifer, Mortimer Smith
and possibly Judge Wood are already in
the field.
There is no lack of candidates for Con
stable of Oakland Township, and it is not
a difficult matter to divine the cause.
Last, year the Constables of Oakland
Township received in fees and mileage
$8354, and this amount does not include all
foes paid for civil business, of which an
enormous quantity Is done in that court.
J. C. Williamson, W. J. Downing and Ed
AV'i'ller have so far put their names for
ward, and as Constable Cronin is after
another job. two of these three will prob
ably be elected.
In a few days it is expected that some
thing will be done by the Democrats and
Populists looking toward the naming of a
county ticket. It is almost certain that
the county will follow the example of the
State and adopt a fusion platform and
programme.
WILL SUPPORT THE TICKET.
There was a meeting of the Young
Men's Republican Club of the Forty-sec
ond Assembly District last evening, at
Turn Verein Hall, the object being to per
fect a strong organization so as to pre
sent a solid front on election day In the
interest of Henry T. Gage, and also to
father the candidacy of J. J. McCarthy
for Assemblyman from the Forty-second
District. The meeting was called to order
by R S. Glaze, who, after stating the ob
ject of the meeting. Invited the members
to present names for their consideration
to act as officers of the club during the
campaign. The following were elected:
R. 8. Glaze, president; Thomas Rothen
berger, vice president; Henry Kelling, re
cording secretary; Charles Birdsall, ser
geant-at-arms. The following executive
committee was appointed by the chair:
Peter L.earch, J. Gilbert, Charles Jami
son John Flaherty and J. T. Harding.
Mr McCarthy, in an address, advised the
members to proffer their services to the
State Central Committee to act as watch
ers during the count on the evening of
election day. and to stay at their post of
duty until the last ticket was counted and
recorded, so as to prevent a repetition of
that political treachery that took place in
some of the districts during the last gu
bernatorial contest.
A CRACK STEEL
SAILING SHIP
The E. M. Phelps the
Best in Her Class.
IS AMERICAN THROUGHOUT
WILL RUN BETWEEN NEW YORK
AND SAN FRANCISCO.
To Load at Baltimore for This Port.
Captain Graham Will Bring
Her Out on Her Maiden
Voyage.
The finest sailing vessel ever built in
America will shortly arrive in San Fran
cisco on her maiden voyage.
The new four-mast ed steel ship Erskine
M. Phelps, built of American material
from stem to stern and from keel to
truck, is now being completed at Bath,
Me. When everything is ready she will
go to Baltimore and there load for thia
port. Her dimensions are: length over
all, 330 feet; length on deep load line, 312
feet; breadth, 40 feet 2 Inches; depth, 25
feet 6 inches. Her gross tonnage is 3000
tons and she has a dead weight carrying
capacity of 4600 tons. The masts average
about 200 feet in height above keelson.
They are 32 inches in diameter in the
partners and 26 inches at heel. The lower
yards are 94 feet long; lower topsail, 86
feet 6 incht-s; upper topsail, 78 feet; top
gallant, 65 feet; royal, 55 feet; and skysail
yards, 46 feet. The bowsprit is uinw of
steel, 6S feet in length.
The ship has two decks and four
hatches, two of the latter being 12x16 feet
and the remaining two Bx6 feet.
The forward deck house is of steel 46
feet long by IS feet wide, In the forward
portion of which is the sailors' cabin,
well lighted and ventilated, with facilities
for heating in cold weather. The rest of
the forward house is devoted to the gal
ley, donkey engine room and coal bunker
of 7 tons capacity. In the midship house,
also of steel, 26x18 feet, is the carpenter's
Shop and six rooms for the petty officers.
Aft under the poop-deck is the commo
dious cabin, tho home of the captain and
his officers, 48 feet long and 38 feet wide,
extending the entire width of the ship.
Those quarters are excellently lighted by
skylights.
In the forward cabin is the dining sa
loon, and aft of this is the main p.i
loon, tastefully finished in white, china
glossed. The captain's apartment, offi
cers' staterooms, steward's pantry, bath
rooms and lavatories are on this deck
and immediately adjoining the saloon.
A stairway leads to a spacious steel
chart house, 14x16 feet, on the upper or
quarter deck, Wi^ numerous side ports
affording abundant light and ventilation.
Here are ample lounging facilities for
passengers or officers when off duty. Aft
of the chart house is the Bteel wheel
house, 10x14 feet.
The ship is supplied with all modern im
provements, including steam pumps and
hose for washing decks or In case of fire.
A steam winch is supplied with two port
able cranes to weigh anchors with, and a
steam capstan is forward and two hand
capstans aft. The bulwarks of the ship
are "stiffened" every four feet with round
iron braces two and one-half inches in di
ameter. Forward are two imported light
houses In which are the sidelights re
quired by law.
The Erskine M. Phelps was built by
Arthur So wall & Co., of Bath, ivlaine, the
well-known builders of the ships Rappa
hannock, Shenandoah (now on her way
to San Francisco), Susquehanna, Roan
oke (the largest wooden merchant vessel
afloat) and the steel ship Dirigo, launch
ed in March, ISM. Arthur Sewall, the
senior member of the firm, was the late
Vice-Presidential candidate on tho Dem
ocratic ticket.
Erskino M. Phelps, for whom the ship
is named, is one of the best known of
Chicago's merchant princes. He is a
prominent member of tho National
Democratic Committee, and during the
recent campaign was one of Mr. Sewall's
strongest supporters.
Captain Robert J. Graham, who will
command the new vessel, is one of the
most popular masters that come to this
port He is an old navigator, and a very
clever business man. When last here
Captain Graham was in command of the
ship W F. Babcock. and was accompanied
by his wife and little daughter, Gladys.
Mrs Graham was one of tmt belles of
Berkeley, and her many friends will be
glad to hear of Captain Graham's pro
motion to the command of one of the
finest sailing vessels afloat.
Captain Graham went to sea when he
was 15 years old in the Bath ship Idle
Lily and gradually worked his way
up in the Sewall employ until he was
master of the ship Reuce. Two years
ago he was given command of the Bab
cock, and now he has been transferred
to the Phelps.
CAUGHT IN THE ACT.
Two Pickpockets Arrested on a
Mission Street Car by the
Harbor Police.
For weeks past the harbor police have
been trying to break up a ganr- of pick
pockets that has made the new ferry
depot its stamping ground. Several ar
rests have been made at different times
and Sunday night Officers Shaw and Bills
locked up Walter Nagle and Frank Wall,
who they think are the last of the band.
It was about 8:30 p. m. and the assengers
by an incoming ferry boat were crowding
into the cars. The policemen had been
watching Wall and Nagle and when they
caw them rush for a Mission-street car
they followed. Wall got on at one end
and in the crush put his hand in a wo
man's pocket, so the officers say. Nagle
got on at the other end but was not
caught In any overt act. Both men
wore placed under arrest. Nagle was and
is a clerk for C. E. Whitney & Co.. pro
duce merchants, 110 Davis street. Nagle's
employes refuse to believe the story told
by the police, and say that for the ; two
years and a half Nagle has been in their
employ he has been sober, honest and in
dustrious. They insist that there is a
mistake somewhere.
Wall was charged with vagrancy at the
City Prison yesterday and released on $100
cash bail. The Nagle case is still in abey-
ance.
The river steamer H. J. Corcoran sus
tained some slight damage when she
came off the drydock yesterday. When
the strain came one of her hog chains
snapped and the other soon followed suit.
The damage will be repaired in a few
hours and the steamer will not be delayed
by the accident.
Pacific-street wharf is back to its old
condition once more and the officers of
the Oceanic Steamship Company are de
lighted. The steamer Australia has got
rid of all the bunks and paraphernalia
that were put in to make her into a
troopship and will ho once again a first
class passenger steamer before she goes
out next Thursday. Chief Steward Pen
field and his assistants have worked won
ders during the last few days, and the
big steamer is once more beginning to
look like herself.
All the vessels of the fleet from Aus
tralia and the Islands are making long
passages. The bark R. P. Rithet that got
in yesterday was thirty-one days and the
schooner Transit was twenty-nine days
from Honolulu. The bark George F.
Manson is now out US days from Sydney,
and 20 per cent reinsurance has been paid
on her, but the chances are that, like
all the others, she is detained by light
winds and calms.
A TIMELY WARNING
TO BOXING CLUBS
FI3HTERS MUST BE IN CONDI
TION AND FLOORS PADDED.
Captain Gillen Refused the Hartford
Club Permission to Carry Out
Its Programme.
The so-called Hartford Boxing Club
pave an entertainment last evening: In a
tire trap on Hartford street.
The ring in which the exponents of the
manly art appeared was several feet less
than the regulation size and the padding
consisted of a cheap blanket.
Sergeant G. B. Griffiths allowed a few
preliminaries as a means or expenmt
but when his good judgment convinced
him that a heavy fall might bo the means
of a death in the family of untrained
sloggers he reported progress to Captain
James W. Gillen; who ordered the festivi
ties to come to a speedy conclusion so as
to avoid possible accident. The directors
of the club were nonplused at the orders
of the captain, but the few prominent
sportsmen who were in attendance eulo
gized the official for his timely Interfer
ence, and they did not hesitate to say
that the captains of the various districts
where hippodrome boxing contests were
held should emulate Captain Gillen s ex
ample and save the lives of poorly condi
tioned boxers by putting a stop to alleged
fistic entertainments.
Mushroom club boxing contests- are or
ganized solely in the interest of a few
rock-rolling politicians who live on the
proceeds of the entertainments to which
they charge an admission fee. The man
agers engage the cheapest fistic < talent
obtainable and as a result some unfortun
ate fellow who is anxious for a cheap rep
utation falls into a trap which may
cause his death.
Had a fire occurred last evening in the
Hartford Club scrapping trap there was
no means of exit < excepting through a
hole-in-the-wall which admitted but one
person at a time. .'"â¢â¢"â  *
Captain Gillen is certainly deserving of
recognition for the stand he has taken In
obliterating these so-called boxing clubs,
whose existence is of only one month's
'rhe contests of the Hartford Club con
sisted of a four-round bout between Jim
Little and Tom O'Leary and a two round
contest between Myers and Sweeney,
which contests were very uninteresting.
IMPORTANT TO DRUGGISTS.
Must Furnish Sworn Statements
Without Delay to the Col
lector.
Collector Lynch received from the Sec
retary of the Treasury yesterday a cir
cular of considerable importance to drug
gists and manufacturers. It is as follows:
in view of the fact that in many cases drug
cists and other dealers In articles subject to
internal revenue tax under schedule B, act of
June 13 1898, were unable to procure the nec
e-^iry revenue stamps for such articles on
hand July 1 IS9S, collectors of internal revenue
will at once notify all such druggists and deal
ers that a sworn Ftatement of all such sales
of unstamped articles made by them on and
after that date should be furnished without de
lay setting forth the dates on which such
sales were made, the quantity (bottles, pack
a*es etc ) so sold and the retail value thereof.
ifoon receipt of the statements so furnished
the tax found to be due will be -reported on
form 23. for assessment.
The penalties imposed by section 20 of the
act named for selling or removing for con
sumption or sale any articles above referred
to without the payment of the tax due will
be enforced In all ciises where the statement
herein required is not furnished with thirty
days from date.

ONR CANNED GOODS ABROAD.
The fruit market continues bare of supplies,
and prices remain high, says the London Gro
cers' Gazette of August 13. A shipment of
over' 16,000 cases has just arrived at Liverpool
per Marlborough Hill, and they were all taken
up and distributed immediately on arrival.
No better reports are to hand from the other
side, and it is stated that packers ixrr paying
forfeit on ;i considerable portion of their con
tracts. Apricots are reported very scarce.
The Salmon market in <iuiet but prices are
unchanged. Reports from the other side speak
of the fishing as being of very unsatisfactory
character, and in consequence of packers re
fraining from selling this season's goods, the
tone of the market has been strengthened, the
few parcels that were offering at easy rates
having been withdrawn. It Is also tinted that
dealers who are not well supplied with stocks
are commencing to buy from those who are
willing to resell on the market. The supply of
half-pound tins is almost exhausted. The Dun
nerdale brought 7465 cases and the Marl
borough Hill and the Caiiforntar. 6616 and 500
cases respectively, the two last named to L'.v
.erpool.

rior of Northern California, Nevada. Utah,

FASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
' XEW YORK. Aug. 29.â Prices of stocks broke
sharply to-day and showed depression through
out. ' The first rally showed considerable vigor.
but later in the day the recuperative power of
the market became feebler and the close was
barely steady at slightly above the lowest. Net
losses ranged from 1 to 2 points in leading
Btocks. There was a growing disposition to ]
realize profits both by professional traders and
by "outside" holders. The latter was shown by
the stead ' stream of selling orders that came
from the commission houses all day. Pro
fessional traders quite generally took that side
of the market and the bears sold with grow
ing confidence, the demand to cover at the
close being very light, even with the sub
stantial reduction of prices. There is evidently
a large growth of the powerful trading ele
ment, which has lightened its holding of stocks
and is anxious for lower prices to buy back
again. There was palpable manipulation at
the opening to fix a low level for prices. In
some of the leading stocks the opening was
about the lowest of the day. Others held quite
firmly for the rest of the day's session, but
eventually yielded to the persistent demon
stration of the bears in other stocks. Buy-
Ing for London account was a distinctive fac
tor in the firmness in a number of international
favorites. There was a steady absorption of
stocks at the decline, which was especially
marked in Burlington and St. Paul. This
seemed to be the result of buying orders below
tne level of the market for trader's account as
â well as from commission houses. The same
thing was evident in the Pacifies and other
â stocks here and throughout the list. The ten
dency to sell was undoubtedly due to the pros
pects of tight money. The minimum rate for
call loans was held at 2 per cent and 3 per cent
was paid in some cases.
The London money market was marked up
a. sharp fraction to-day and there was buying
of gold in the open market there, said to bo
for shipment to New York. No engagement

LONDON MARKETS.
. NEW YORK. Aug. 29.â Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says: The settle
ment here shows an Increased account, not
ably In Americans, but money is so plentiful
that eontangoes were quite light, those on
Americans being only 2% per cent. The tone
of the markets was generally good to-day on
the â¦Tzar's universal peace manifesto. A fur
ther rise in Brazils and Argentines was the
chief feature. Americans were irregular, some
of the active shares reacting on profit taking,
but the close was generally better. For the
moment I am able to report considerable pub
lic buying here, but whether it will last it is
difficult to say. St. Paul stock was scarce at
the settlement and there was no contango.
New York Is now buying gold freely. I learn
over 400.000 pounds has been bought. The Lon
don discount rate Is 1% per cent In consequence
and IS per cent is asked for American bills
for forward delivery.

CHICAGO GRAIN MARKET.
CHICAGO. Aug. 29.â Wheat experienced a
strong start because of disappointingly small
receipts in the Northwest and higher quota
tions from Liverpool, although the latter were
not unexpected in view of the advance on this
side Saturday. Visible supply statement showed
77 000 bushels increase for the week. Asa de
crease i had been expected the bullishness of
the market received a partial extinguisher.
People who needed September wheat and had
found considerable difficulty in buying were
now enabled to have their wants supplied with
out shouting for the wheat, sellers betas com
pelled to take a turn at the shouting. TV? esti
mate that carloads of wheat at Chicago nor to
morrow's inspection would be 406 was anVher
blow to the bulls in the market. That in\d
dition to the disappointment about the vlsltJlo
caused September to drop for a time all its
early advance. December was relatively firmer
and did not decline more than He, while Sep
tember was losing Ic. December was much
more largely dealt in than September, and has
most pronouncedly become the active trading
future. September was difficult to buy when
wanted and hard to sell when that was con
sidered desirable. September left off %tf%c
higher, and December gained *4@%e.
A good business was done in corn and In the
main the market was strong. The bone of
contention was the condition of the growing
crop upon which subject there was a wide dif
ference of opinion. The close Is unchanged to
%c up. .
There was. little done in oats, and the close
was unchanged on the day.
Moderate buying by English houses held
provisions firm. Lard and ribs were compara
tively stronger than pork. September hold
ings have been transferred or sold, and â  the
trade was no longer under the influence of op
â¢ erations by people who had no us*> for pro
visions on delivery day. Pork and lard are un
changed and ribs rose 7%<9>10c.

HAY AND FEEDSTUFFS.
Yesterday's receipts of Hay were the larg
est ever known, being 1654 tons. There were
twenty-five schooners and sloops and one
barga. besides a large number of cars. The
farmers are afraid of early rains and are rush
ing their holdings in. Those, however, who
have storage capacity are generally holding
on. The heavy receipts produced no effect
Whatever on prices.

NOTE In the above exposition of the tides
the early morning tides are glvpn In the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column Rives the second tide ot
the day. the third time column the third tide
and the' last or right hand column gives the
last tide of the day. except when there are but
three tides, as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sU*i (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference is the mean of the Inwcr tow waters.

NOTICE TO MARINERS.
A branch of the United States Hydrographio
Office, located In the Merchants" Exchange la
maintained In San Francisco for the benefit of
mariners, without regard to nationality and
free of expense.
Navigators are cordially Invited to visit the
office, where complete sets of charts and sail
ing directions of the world are kept on hand
for comparison and reference, "and the latest
Information can always be obtained regarding
lights, dangers to navigation and all matters
of Interest to ocean commerce.
The time ball on the tower of the new Ferry
building, at the foot of Market street, is hoisted
about ten minutes before noon and dropped at
noon. 120 th meridian, by telegraphic signal re
ceived each day from the United States Signal
Observatory, Mare Island, Cal.
A notice stating whether the ball was dropped
on time or giving the error, if any. 1Â« published
in the morning papers the following day
_ , CHAS. P. WELCH,
Ensign (retired). U. S. N., In charge.

and was sensational to a degree when it
r- ferred to the disposition and conduct of
Mrs. Scott. As reviewed by the court in
the decision, "Mrs. Soott was a woman
â¢.. endowed with physical form of symmet
rical-proportions, with stately presence
and digniiied carriage, conscious of her
Charms, â  proud and vain of her beauty
and avaricious of admiration and jealous
' of attention bestowed upon others of her
sex, of whom she was not fond, having
. . little confidence in the virtue of women
and less faith in woman's virtue." Nev
ertheless, she was of sound mind, holds
the court, and was capable of disposing
of her property as her conscience die
. tated.
At the time of his marriage Scott was
In .financial distress. He, for some time,
had met with but little success in busi
ness, and new duties devolved upon him
when he came to manage his wife's es
tate, and as such manager he was not a
; success. The court, on the other hand,
.: finds that Mrs, Scott was learned in busi
methods and shrewd. It was nat
ural under the circumstances that she
should have been disappointed, states
Judge Coffoy, in finding that her 1; I
' was lacking in business ability. Her abil
ity and her disappointment that her hus
band was not so gifted are strong cvi- j
dences of her sanity. As to hÂ«r personal
'ttatnre, the court found that she was
coarse, oi.se^-ne and immodest. Although
â¢. â¢ proud and stately in public bearing,
. :ln.'her- home, states the court, "she
threw off hi r affe< tation of r"s< rye and
â ' reticence and assumed an abandon of act
â¢" and expression that would put Billings
gate' to blush and cause Its fishmongers
â  to hide their heads in shame." She used
;â¢ obscene language, indulged in nude gam
..:. .'tols before her mirror and was guilty of
â¢ departure from modesty in many ways,
â  but the court refused to consider such
â¢ characteristics as evidence of insanity.
â¢.. Another point in her favor was that she
had reason to believe that h<;r husband
was unfaithful to her, and reference was
â  made to an occasion when she caught her
husband fondling a servant girl.
' :'. The will also is strong evidence of the
sanity of the testatrix. She disposed of
her property in a natural way. remember
ing relatives of her first husband as well
as those of her second. She left her hus
band that portion that pleased her mind,
and that it did not please her husband is
no reason for setting it aside, Conse
quently, the will was sustained.
S^ott. it is believed, will take an appeal
to the Supreme Court.

OFFER OF A
CAMP GROUND
AT HONOLULU
Not Far From the
Business Center.
IS SUPPLIED WITH WATER
TROUBLES OF THE SEVENTH
CALIFORNIA REGIMENT.
Another Emphatic Denial of an
Epidemic of Typhoid Fever Is
Made by the
Officers.
Just before his departure for Honolulu
General Merriam received a letter from
Colonel Barber stating that it was a dif
ficult matter to find suitable camping
grounds there for the troops that were to
be sent to the islands. A day or two later
there came instructions from Washington
revoking the order to forward troops to
Honolulu until the return of General Mer
riam, who would report upon a camp site.
When General Merriam comes back
from the islands, In about two weeks,
whether or not he has discovereu any
camping ground in or near Honolulu, he
will find awaiting him here an offer of a
suitable site. The offer is made by CL.
Wllcox, a. prominent business man of
Honolulu and a member of the Hawaiian
Senate. Mr. Wilcox, who is indorsed by
Senator Perkins, consulted with Colonel
Berry of the Seventh California in regard
to the matter and at the Colonel's sugges
tion he put his proposition in writing and
addressed it to General Merriam. It is
as follows:
Understanding that the forwarding of troops
to Honolulu, as ' contemplated, has been inter
fere! with because doubts have been created
as to securing a suitable camp ground, I would
respectfully submit that I am the owner of a
tract of land within two miles of the business
center of Honolulu, It being the unoccupied
portion of a block, and containing- about NO
l.y 4UO feet, of land adjacent to and bounded
by Sheridan and King streets, along one of
w hlch a Government water pipe Is laid, which
I hereby offer for use as a temporary camp
ground, from one to six months, at the option
of the Government, for any troops the United
States Government may desire 'to fjfward
there: the only condition being that when the
ground Is vacated by the troops it be placed
In as good condition as when the/ took pos
session of It.
NO EPIDEMIC OF TYPHOID.
Officers of thÂ© Seventh Califor
nia Again Make an Em
phatic Denial.
Regarding the article published In yes
terday's Chronicle in relation to the in
crease of typhoid fever in the Seventh
California Regiment, and the statement
that Major Choate and his assistants were
diagnosing the cases as measles, when
they were fever, in order to su-nress the
seriousness of the malady, Major Mat
thews, surgeon in charge of the division
hospital, makes the following statement:
"I want to say once and for the last
time that no epidemic of typhoid fever
exists in the Seventh California Regiment.
The percentage of sickness in the regi
ment is small. We have here the healthi
est army corps in the United States ser
vice. There is no reason Â£or the daily
publication of the sensational articles
that have been appearing, for there is no
truth in them. Tue statement published
by the Chronicle that the medical officers
of this hospital said that the surgtons of
the Seventh Regiment were diagnosing
cases as measles when they were fever is
not true, as that statement was not made
by myself or any of my officers. Let me
tell the public that there have been in the
Division Hospital two deaths from ty
phoid fever. The others have been most
ly from pneumonia and a complication of
other diseases with typhoid fever. The
fact that the hospital is so crowded is be
cause we are sending no patients away
now, and not that there is any alarming
â increase in any disease. These are the
facts in the case and there is no cause
for such alarm as the sensational articles
now being published would indicate. I
have given the facts to The Call once be
fore and I repeat them again so that the
public may know the truth."
Colonel Berry makes the following
statement: "I want to say through the
columns of The Call, for it is the paper
that has stood for decency and reliabil
ity ever since the war began, that these
articles that are being published in the
Chronicle, in which it is daily asserted
that my regiment is suffering from an
epidemic of typhoid fever and that my
medical officers are perjuring themselves
by diagnosing the cases as one thing
when they are another, is a personal at
tack upon me. The report that the regi
ment was kept at Camp Merritt when
the men were dying of fever, simply for
the re.-ison that we wanted to get away
to Honolulu, and that the fact that fever
was raging in the command was kept a
secret from General Miller is absolutely
false. I myself, at the suggestion of
Major Choate, went twice to see General
Miller and told him that if we were not
to be sent away the regiment should
be moved at once, as there was some
fever among the men, and I thought that
it was due to some local trouble.
"By no means all of the fever cases that
have been sent from the regiment are
typhoid; many of them are malarial with
tvphold symptoms, as will be borne out
by the reports of the division hospital
surgeons. Knowing that The Call has
been reliable in all of its statements and
that it has taken no notice of the yellow
sensations of the camp, but has simply
told the truth, I make this statement
because I want the public to know the
facts.
"The percentage of sickness in the regi
ment is very small. At present it is leas
than 10 per cent, while frequently it has
been the case that regiments have had
over 30 per cent of sickness, and not
much was thought of it."
THE "HOODOO" REGIMENT.
Little Chance of the Seventh
Seeing Active Service.
It looks as though fate was against the
Seventh Regiment of California Volun
teers and that it is destined to pass Into
history as the "Hoodoo Regiment." Its
members were among the first to enlist
in response to the call of their country,
but bo far they have only had the oppor
tunity of lighting measles and typhoid
fever In the most unhealthful place on the
peninsula. The Chamber of Commerce
urged the President of the United States
to do what he could to give the boys a
chance at the front and the answer re
ceived yesterday indicates that the sol
j diers of the Seventh are to become per
j manent residents of this city, the follow
' ing dispatch coming from the War De-
I partmtnt, which gives little or no encour
agement of active service:
Hugh Craigâ Sir: Acknowledging the receipt
of your letter to the President or the 29th
ultimo. In which you say that the Seventh
California Regiment of Infantry was the first
to leave their homes for the volunteer service
of the United States and are greatly disap
pointed at being held In California so long,
while Kastern regiments are being sent to the
front, the Secretary of War desires me to In
form you that the Dejiurtment does not at
present contemplate the sending of any troops
further WeÃt than Honolulu, Sandwich Isl
ands. Very respectfully,

"On going Into the office the clerk, said

in our water bill we would like definite
figures.' â  . I ,:'

TO BUILD A NEW SCHOOL,
The Teachers' War at Claremont to
Be Waged to the Bitter End.
OAKLAND, Aug. 29.â Perhaps never has
a more determined fight been waged to
upset politics in a school board than that
which has almost been brought to a head
In Claremont,
To-day the public school teacher. Miss
Landstrom,' taught but seven children,
while her rival, Miss Mintle Gerow,
taught twenty-one In the tent under the
trees near the entrance to the Ainsworth
place. The school in the tent is to be lo
cated in more permanent quarters. Trus
tee Logan, who is the only friend on the
board of three favorable to Miss Gerow,
has consulted with the parents of the
children in her class, and they have de
cided to erect at once a commodious
schoolhouse. The plans have been pre
pared, and in a few weeks the building
will be ready.

Home for Convalescents.
OAKLAND, Aug. 29.â The Oakland Red
Cross Society has opened a convalescent
home for soldiers on Piedmont avenue,
opposite Keller's nursery. The necessity
for this action was so urgent that the
committee met yesterday and arranged
to receive the convalescent soldiers to
day. Some of these soldiers are so sick
and weak they can hardly walk, yet had
not Oakland opened her doors to them
they would have had to sleep on the
ground.

WRIGHT GETS NOTHING.
Must Pay Costs of Suit Against the
Oil Works.
OAKLAND, Aug. 29.â A remittitur has
been returned from the State Supreme
Court reversing the decision of the Supe
rior Court in the damage suit of W. H.
Wright against the Pacific Coast Oil Com
pany. Wright was given judgment for
$1170, and defendant argued for a new J
trial, but it was denied. The case was ap- j
pealed and has hung fire for two years.
By the decision of the Supreme Court
W right's judgment is rendend void and
he is compelled to pay costs of appeal.
Wright was un employe at the oil works
in Alameda. His duty was to attend the
thirteen oil vats on the place. One night
in November, 1535, a vat sprung a leak
and the boiling oil poured out upon him,
incapacitating him from work.

so that it required a surgical operation
